            "statement": "eBay's proxy-bidding algorithm does not simply add a fixed increment each time a bidder is outbid; instead, whenever a new bid is submitted, the visible current price jumps to the minimum of (a) the new bidder's maximum, or (b) the previous leader's maximum plus the minimum bid increment.           {
            "statement": "Economic research by Roth and Ockenfels found that late/last-second bidding ('sniping') is a rational, surplus-maximizing strategy specifically in hard-close proxy-bid auctions (like eBay) because it prevents rival bidders from reacting to the information leaked by an early proxy bid — i.e., sniping functionally converts an ascending proxy auction back into something closer to a sealed-bid (Vickrey-style) auction. Their empirical analysis found winning bidders are disproportionately likely to have placed a single bid very late in the auction, and this incentive to snipe is much weaker or absent in auctions with automatic time-extension ('soft close') endings.",

            "statement": "Standard anti-snipe/auction-extension mechanics work by automatically extending the countdown by a fixed or platform-defined window every time a new bid lands within that window of the scheduled close, repeating until no further bids arrive in time — this deliberately keeps the auction 'open' during what would otherwise be the snipe window so other watching bidders can respond. A documented bypass/mitigation nuance is that if the extension length is fixed and disclosed, bidders can still time a bid to land just outside the trigger window; some platforms counter this by randomizing or not disclosing the exact extension duration, forcing bidders toward bidding true valuations early rather than gaming the exact reopening threshold.",

            "statement": "A documented race condition in an open-source bid-placement system (18F/micropurchase, used for U.S. government micro-procurement auctions) occurs when the code path reads the current winning-bid amount via `current_bid_amount`, then performs validation against that cached value before writing the new bid — but if a second bidder's request completes and writes a new winning bid in the gap between the read and the write, the first request's validation runs against stale data and can incorrectly accept (or reject) a bid that is no longer valid relative to the true current price. The proposed fix was optimistic locking on the Auction record plus wrapping the check-and-write in a database transaction so a concurrent modification forces a retry instead of silently succeeding on stale data.",

            "statement": "In an audited NFT auction smart contract (NextGen 'AuctionDemo'), a boundary-condition bug combined two flaws into an exploitable race condition: the contract used non-strict inequalities (>= / <=) for both the 'bidding open' and 'claiming open' phase checks, so at the exact block timestamp equal to the auction's end time, both phases were simultaneously active. Combined with a missing status check in `claimAuction`, an attacker could place a bid at that exact timestamp, win the auction, call `claimAuction` to get a refund on a non-winning bid, and then also call `cancelBid` on the same bid for a second, duplicate refund — allowing the attacker to receive the NFT for free while draining ETH deposited by other, unrelated concurrent auctions on the same contract. Auditors noted this exact-timestamp collision had an estimated ~99.98% chance of occurring across 100 concurrent Ethereum auctions, i.e., it was near-certain to be hit in production, not a theoretical edge case.",

        "angle": "proxy-bid-norms",
        "angle_summary": "Researched via web search/fetch of eBay's own help/community content, other auction platforms' published bidding rules, and direct GitHub code search (via gh CLI) across ~20 open-source auction repos plus one production Solidity protocol. Finding: the \"max bid can only be raised, never lowered\" behavior is real and consistently documented for eBay-style consumer proxy bidding, but it is a business/policy rule enforced procedurally (via retraction restrictions), not a universal architectural law of \"proxy bidding\" as a pattern. Most open-source reference implementations do NOT enforce raise-only semantics in their update code — they simply overwrite/upsert the stored max bid on each call, validating only that it exceeds the current price/highest bid, not that it exceeds the bidder's own prior max. Only one clear counter-example of raise-only enforcement in code was found, and it was in a non-eBay-style context (a DeFi grant-pool \"max bid\" cap, not competitive consumer bidding).",

        "angle": "fraud-detection-realism",
        "angle_summary": "The research converged clearly: academic literature is rich with sophisticated statistical/ML shill-bidding detection algorithms (Local Outlier Factor, Markov Random Fields, 'Live Shill Score'), but every one of these is validated only on scraped/simulated data, never on documented small-operator production deployments — researchers themselves note real auction sites won't share data. The one solid real-world case study found (Trade Me/Auto Co, NZ) was resolved via a trust-and-safety-team investigation triggered by noticing a suspicious pattern of $1-reserve listings, using account/IP matching — closer to manual/heuristic investigation than automated statistical flagging, though Trade Me is larger than the \"small operator\" scope asked about. Practitioner-level advice for small/independent auction sites (forums, auctioneer blogs) is explicitly manual and heuristic: eyeball bid patterns, check repeat bidder-seller pairings, crowdsource flagging from regular bidders. Independent (non-vendor) sources like FraudPractice.com and even vendor-authored content concede manual review is standard and recommended specifically at low transaction volumes, since automated tooling costs aren't justified. A large-sample Forrester/Experian survey found 70% of ecommerce retailers still rely on manual review, though that sample skews toward organizations big enough to have formal 'fraud teams,' likely understating true small-operator manual/informal reliance. Nearly all \"small business fraud detection\" search results returned vendor marketing content (Signifyd, Riskified, NoFraud, Kount, etc.) with an obvious incentive to oversell automation — this was flagged explicitly as low-confidence/high-bias-risk. Net conclusion: the specific mechanism the user asked about (automated statistical flagging of a fine-grained pattern like consistent win-margin) is a research-paper construct, not a documented small-operator practice; real-world small/mid operator fraud catching is manual, complaint/escalation-driven, and heuristic, consistent with the user's hypothesis.",
